MRM1284	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1487	L		1851-07-20					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Boner, Charles"	Three Mile Cross		Kelley et al. 1988(17):268; Kettle 1871(1):210-212	"I do not know, dear Mr. Boner, whether, when I last wrote.  as Mrs. Browning has advanced as far as Paris towards London, I wait to know whether she really does mean to come to England this year before making up my mind to take the journey without the chance of seeing her. Unless she comes, I dont think I shall get to London.  Mrs. Browning sent me her book. It is a dull tirade on Italian politics. When I say dull and yet vigorously written, that sounds like a contradiction, but it is not so. The subject, which is not largely though forcibly treated, is so unreal that it excites no sympathy, for it seems to me out of the question that a people without recent poetry, without living literature, without even an attempt at eloquence,whose last great writer was Alfieri, whose sculpture has dwindled into wood-carving, whose pictorial art into mosaic copies, who have not even produced one man of mark in this general tossing up of nations, should be ripe for freedom and self-government. Year by year they seem to me dwindling. Even music, which held by them longest, is now dying away. They have still singers, but they have no composers. Italy is an extinct volcano. The very smoke is gone, and it seems to me wrong as well as foolish to try to provoke a struggle, which can only end in the reaction always so fatal to progress and rational liberty. Dont you agree with me? There are one or two tolerable passages, but the metre is harsh and unattractive, and the triple rhyme of Dante quite unsuited to English verse; and the only result of the book will be a bill at Chapman and Halls, and a total exclusion from Italy for the writer in case she wants to return thither. It will make no hit here. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2149/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

I went to London on purpose to meet hersaw her nearly every daywe were excellent friends& the result is that from the moment we parted I have never heard a syllable of any one of them. It is likely that I offended Mr Kenyon by refusing two invitationsbut what I have done to be sent to a sort of epistolary Coventry by the whole party I cant conjecture Wrote to Mrs Browning after I got home& so it shall remain unless she writes to me I shall make no quarrel of it but I have been too much attached to Mrs Browning to pass into a common acquaintance or a literary friendthat coterie of authors to whom her husband belongs is the worst Society in London. I never would have anything to do with it, & that I suppose is my real sin. In the meanwhile I have plenty to do here God knows. A strange thing it seemed to see her walking about like other people. She and her husband are now gone to winter in Paris. I talk of meeting them there in the spring. They have a pretty little boy, but it was odd to hear the English parents and the English nurse talking to him in Italian. I suppose next year they will all talk to him in French, and when English will take its turn, God knows. In their way to England they stopped at Paris, and in the Louvre they thought they saw Alfred Tennyson: looking at the book they found his name written A. Tennyson, Rentier. (Is not that curious?) So then they met and offered each other their houses, he having a cottage at Twickenham, and they not having given up their apartment at the Casa Guidi. By the way, I dont think they can return to Florence with that book, the Casa Guidi Windows, against them. When I was in London I heard from three quarters that Mazzini wants to have it translated as a political pamphlet. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2179/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

Well, he had painted a portrait of me, far bigger than life, and with equal excess of colour, but otherwise like. My father, however, had not praised it enough to please him. His nice taste had found that he disliked it. So it had been kept by the artist, and he had cut out the head. This head was amongst the things sent to Miss Barrett. He gave it to her, he said, because he knew she would value it. The next day he called again in Wimpole Street, to say that he could not part with that portrait, he would only lend it to her. This was three days before his death. You may imagine how it touched me when I heard of it. By-the-way, I find this morning that this portrait, now in the possession of his and my friend Mr. Barrett, is about to be engraved for the Tragedies. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2218/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

Mrs. Browning says that the courage and activity shown in the coup détat have never been surpassed. She tells some capital stories of Emile de Girardin, and says that the Prince says of himself, that his life will have had four phasesone all rashness and inprudence, necessary to make his name known, and to make his own faults known to himself; the next the combat with, and triumph over, anarchy; the third the consolidation of France and pacification of Europe; the last un coup de pistolet. The passion of parties is so excited, that the only thing which renders the last improbable is the sort of fate by which men of that very high and calm courage often escape dangers by braving them.  Mrs. Browning says it is certain that Dumas four hundred or five hundred volumes are all written by his own hand, like the Prince Presidents decrees. MRM1297	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1549	L		1852-02-19					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Jephson, Emily E."	Swallowfield		Kelley et al. 1988(18):358-359; LEstrange 1882(2):173-175	"I do not know, my very dear Emily, that I ever received more pleasure than from a most kind and charming letter from that delightful brother-in-law of yours, Mr. Starkey.  To-day I received, too, another letter from Mrs. Browning; she has seen George Sand, and is charmed with her. She came to Paris chiefly to solicit the President for a friend of hers. He received her most kindly, shook hands with her, and granted her request. (Those precious newspapers of ours said, if you remember, that he had exiled her.) Mrs. Browning is quite as enthusiastic for the Prince President as ever. To-day, too, came a packet of unbound sheetsa poem without a title-page, whose title I take to be Verdicts. It is on recent poets, and shows great boldness and talent. The page and a half given to me is most carefully and beautifully written, and with so much encomium that one cannot help thinking it must be by a friend. Mrs. Browning says George Sand is not taller than I am,short, of a colourless, olive complexion, with dark, glowing eyes, black hair, and a noble countenance. She was very simply dressed (as a woman), in a room with a bed in ither manner very kind, very quiet; a low, soft voice, an unemphatic utterance, rather calm than ardent. Now all this I knew except the shortness, which I can hardly believe even now; she must have looked so ill in doublet and hose. Mrs. Browning could not help stooping to kiss her hand, upon which Madame Sand threw her arms round her neck and kissed her upon the lips. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2240/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

I have a great love of French literature, and a great habit of throwing myself into it for months together, and the lyrics of Victor Hugo (not his dramas or novels), and the chansons of the great old man (of which I do not mean the chansons griroises, for which he is now probably infinitely grieved) always seem to me the verdant spots of French poetry. Browning says M. de Cormenin (you know the brilliant political writer) was Louis Napoleons adviser in the confiscation of the Orleans property. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2245/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

She might hear it& she is so sensitive sometimes that it might stop the spring which I would regret much Yes Ill send you more of her letters & now you shall have a bit of her last She is a most charming person very like those letters in a general way but with fits of morbid sentiment between whilesstill a most dear & most charming creature She told me in her last letter but one that Louis Napoleon was told by a black woman at Ham that he should rule over Franceshould make her great & happyshould pacify Europe& then die of a pistol shot in a ball room This the person (a friend of the Prince-President) who told Mrs Browning said he firmly believedbeing like most imaginative persons a thorough fatalist In her last she said that the confiscation of the Orleans property was advised by M. de CormeninTimon you know, by far the most brilliant of French political writers Of course you have read his Orateurs She also says a very philosophically minded man (French) was talking of these things the other dayone of the most thoughtful liberal men I ever knew & much more anglomane than I amalso let me add high & pure in his moral views He was talking of the English press. He said he did it justice for good & noble intentions (more than I do) but marvelled at its extraordinary ignorance. Those writers do not know the ABC of France. Then as to Louis Napoleon whether he was right or wrong they erred in supposing him not to be in earnest in his constitution & other remedies for Francehe not only is sincere he is fanatical So far Mrs Browning. MRM1457	Reading Central Library	qB/TU/MIT	Horizon No.: 1361554	SD1564	L		1852-04-07					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Ouvry, Mrs. Jane"	Swallowfield		Kelley et al. 1988(18):362-363; Chorley 1872(2):128-131	"My very dear Mrs Ouvry So entirely do I join with you in condemning Madame Sand that I point blank refused Mrs Browning to send her my books with hers some years ago Those years have a good deal altered the face of things She has reformed her bad wayslives quietly with her children& above all has given up the demoralising book writing which was far more mischievous than her personal immorality & puts her genius (for it is more than talent) to its proper use  Her later stories & above all her exquisite rustic dramas give token of a greatly altered moral sensein short she is now a person whom I should not object to meet although I should not go in search of her, & should most assuredly never dream of any hand kissing. Beranger & the Prince-President are the only two personages in France whose hands I have any fancy to shake But Mrs Browning always did make an Idol of Talent The thing that astounds me is that that propensity should not have vanished after seeing the clay feetas she must have done in so many cases  My friend Mr. Bennett who besides being a charming poet is an eminent watch maker & jeweller gave me the other day a most curious account of the Forger of the Shelley Letters. A young man quite sufficiently like Lord Byron to vindicate his claim to illegitimacy called on him a year or two ago to bespeak a locket for three curls of his fathers hair cut off at three different periods of his life (I dare say they were hair forgeries, cuttings from wigs) He called himself Mr Byron & coming often to see about the locket one day asked Mr Bennett to accompany him home to examine certain papers & engravings which he had been collecting for the purpose of a great life of his father which he had in contemplation  No doubt amongst these were taken the Shelley forgeries& there doubtless were the germs of many others They would Mr Bennett says have deceived the whole world as indeed they did deceive not only Robert Browning probably no great judge, but practised publishers like Mr Moxon & Mr Murrayautograph collectors & autograph auctioneers. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2260/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

MRM1304	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1569	L		1852-04-21					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Pearson, Hugh"	Swallowfield		Kelley et al. 1988(18):363-364; Chorley 1872(2):200-201	"I said I would find the missing letter, and I didrather sooner than I expected, and of course when hunting for something else. They all laugh at my enthusiasm, and they are welcome to do so. What else could keep glowing in me, poor old cripple that I am, left alone in the world after a life of wear and tear, all that forms the solace and the delight of my existencethe love of nature and the love of poetry, the power of cultivating new friendships as well as old ones, and of enjoying to my hearts core such an hour as I spent yesterday? The amusing part of this is, that she, who pretends to be so calm and cool an observer is just as enthusiastic as I who make no such vain pretenceonly that dear friend of mine is more carried away by authorship, by mere pen and inkand yet this last account of Madame Sand is a disenchantment if ever there were one. I made sure of your being charmed by Mrs. Brownings letters. I have a great basket full of such, for before Mr. Browning stole her from me we used to write to each other at least twice a week, and by dint of intimacy and frequency of communication could, I think, have found enough matter for a correspondence of twice a day. It was really talk, fireside talk, neither better nor worse, assuming necessarily a form of permanencegossip daguerreotyped. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2267/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

If I have, I suppose that some time or other I shall make a volume of tragedies old and new, for my very best Otto of Wittelsbach, is in a similar predicamentat all events, the whole will be printed after I am dead. I have most interesting accounts of the Prince President from Mrs. Browning and Mr. Fields, whom I expect here soon, and who will, I hope, bring me a cartload of books about him. I told him to get me all, and the best portraits. He wrote to me last summer that the womens heads were good for nothing but to stick flowers in, and the mens to hang beards to; and, at the bottom of her heart, I expect Mrs. Browning to have the same feeling. Over and over she has said of them to me, My Italians are too soft. That book, the Casa Guidi Windows, is a book without convictionsone feels that as one reads it. They are to stay at Florence till March and then proceed to Rome and Naples. She speaks wretchedly of the state of Italybut magnificently of France. I have seen three letters from Paris to-day, and they say as she says that never was it so gay or so prosperous and that war is altogether out of the questionNapoleon III is too wise; for it is the one thing that would ruin him. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2362/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

MRM1403	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1730	L		1854-04-22					"Ruskin, John James"	"Mitford, Mary Russell"			Kelley et al. 1988(20):366; LEstrange 1882(2):119120	"Saturday Evening, Dear Miss Mitford, I have just finished Atherton, to my great regret, thinking it one of the sweetest things you have ever written, and receiving from it the same kind of refreshment which I do from lying on the grass in spring.  April 23rd.I have had one other feast, however, this Sunday morning in your dear friends poemsElizabeth Browning. I have not had my eyes so often wet for these five years. I had no conception of her power before. I cant tell you how wonderful I think them. I have been reading the Valediction, and the Years Spinning, and the Reed, and the Dead Pan, and the Dead Baby at Florence, and the Caterina to Camoens, and all for the first time! I only knew her mystical thingsyounger, I supposebefore. Tuesday.I kept this to put another sheet, but cant keep it longer. Yours gratefully J. Ruskin"	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2512/?rsId=16346&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

MRM1408	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1761	L		1854-06-23					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Jephson, Emily E."	Swallowfield		Kelley et al. 1988(20):373; LEstrange 1882(2):268-274	"I am delighted to hear again from you, my beloved Emily, above all to receive a letter so cheerful and so full of health, mental and bodily.  For the rest people are exceedingly good, for even the troublesome mean, I believe, to be kind, and little Miss Mary is a great comfort and delight. Your charming description of Miss Emily would almost serve for hershe is the brightest, merriest, happiest creature that ever existedknowing fewer words, I think, than six months ago. You know she was a year old the second of last January, but everybody takes her for a twelvemonth older, she is so tall, so large, and so active, understanding everybody, and making herself understood in spite of her want of language. Such a mimic never was seen. She comes to my door knocking with her little clenched fist every time she can escape from her father and mother and the maid, and in imitation, we suppose, of her brother, folds her little hands every night and says, Bless papa and mamma and poor Ba, the hideous name (nobody can guess why) she will call me.  Mr. Fields was to have been here in England this month, but his visit is deferred till the next, and I have some notion will not take place this year. This is only my feeling, for he says he is coming. So say the Brownings from Italy, and Mr. Boner from Germany. The little Browning boy suffered from malaria at Rome, but is, I think, recovering."	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2548/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

MRM0035	Berkshire Record Office	D/EZ 131/8/1-3		SD1769	L		1854-08-09				env	"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Bennett, William Cox "	Swallowfield	"Cheapside, 65, London"	Kelley et al. 1988(20):375	"August 9th in the evening. I must have seemed most ungrateful in never noticing the P.S. of your  Mrs. Brownings address is Alla Signora Browning, Poste Restante, Firenze, Toscana, Italia, You may add in one line at top FlorenceTuscanyItaly P.S. I earnestly hope my dear friend that you have no intention to mention money matters in any way to Mrs Browningmuch less to offer them any loan They would never forgive you or mebeing amongst the touchiest people possible & standing the most (he particularly who has no sort of claim to it) upon position Indeed she herself thinks more of Lords & Ladies than you can imagine& I have observed that they who run so much after the more fashionable names in literature, commonly run after those other names of the court calendara sad weaknessMrs Archer Clive does it& Mrs Acton Tindal& others whom we both know & who ought to know better However they would never pardon you nor me for telling you although of course in writing to so dear a friend as you it was in confidence & there was no hint of secrecy in her letter. Moreover the time is past It is too late to come to England this year or even to go to Lucca. I put you upon honour as to thisthough I rather hope that you want her leave to insert portions of her poems in a book of Extracts of which Mr Bennoch once spoke to me. Why did not you talk about it when here? It is by extracts that Mrs Browning will liveher husband will never in my opinion survive the present school The obscurity and incompleteness that disfigure her poems are hidden in that mode of publication, & the magnificent genius which shines out in other parts appears in full lustre.  Includes comments on recipient's poems ""I doubt if blank verse & on a Greek subject will take root like English rhymed stanzas"""	http://www.nationalarchives.gov.uk/a2a/records.aspx?cat=005-dez&cid=116-1-8#116-1-8	9/21/2013	GHB

MRM0037	Berkshire Record Office	D/EZ 131/9/1-2		SD1774	L		1854-09-13				env	"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Bennett, William Cox "	Swallowfield	"Cheapside, 65, London"	Kelley et al. 1988(20):380	"Wednesday, My beloved friendI am really better todaywhich means that your kind visit did me as much good as beyond all doubt it gave me pleasure.  The morning brought me an exquisite letter from Mr Harness.  It also brought a very affectionate one from Mrs Browning. The laziness of her husband is something to wonder at She & the poor puny delicate boy who at three years old would not have made half of one girl at 20 months have been kept broiling in Florence all the summer (& you know that nobody stays in Florence during the hot weather) because they had not money enough to move even so far as the Baths of Lucca Well! he does nothing & nothing will he door try& Mr Horne (a perfectly truthful person) told me that he had frequently offered him workreviewingextractingcompiling & such like & he had refused it. I had enquired about Mr Kenyon & I find that he does allow them £100 a year (think of a man accepting that sum in charity when he could earn it)but that, that £100 included, their income is under £300 Now I know that she had at her marriage £10,000 as legacy from her uncle, so the rest of the interest must either be paid for old debts, or the sum must have been deducted at that time before it was re-settledeither way most discreditable to him. I think you do not now wonder at the little I think of him as a dramatist Her letter is most affectionatebut there is a great lowering of tone since her marriage  Includes comments on the cleaning of her sickroom"	http://www.nationalarchives.gov.uk/a2a/records.aspx?cat=005-dez&cid=116-1-9#116-1-9	9/21/2013	GHB

MRM0023	Berkshire Record Office	D/EZ 131/14/1-130		SD1775	L		1854-09-18					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Bennett, William Cox "	Swallowfield		Kelley et al. 1988(20):380	"Typescript at Berkshire Record Office: Your most kind letter my very dear friend arrived with 9 others and a packet  Robert Browning has no sense of duty (It was I who told you of those two books to be) I suggested to his wife that they might make use of their reputation by writing proseas I did Mr. Barrett has had a terrible accidentgetting out of an omnibus he was run over by a cabriolet and something broken about the hip which cannot be set, and although it brings no present danger to life, will leave him uncurably and terribly lame. This will change all his habits. He used to go into the city every day having made his great West India Estates profitable by being his own Merchant and his own shipowner His children are most attentive and he bears the calamity with great fortitude. His daughter ought to return to Englandbut I doubt their doing soshe should take the chance a circumstance finding forgiveness. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2572/?rsId=16387&nav=next	5/19/2014	GHB

MRM1438	"Published, MS location unspecified"			SD1795	L		1854-12-16					"Mitford, Mary Russell"	"Harness, William"	Swallowfield	Kensington Gore	LEstrange 1870(3):303-304	"My dear friend, Judge Edmonds book arrived Saturday afternoon, just before Messrs. Pearson and Bennoch, and I sent it off on Monday by Mr. Chorley. Before this time Mr. May had called; and I wish you had seen how much these two men (I mean Mr. May and Mr. Pearson), both accustomed to death-beds, were frightened to find me reading it. Of course they did not suspect me of believing, but they say that the hold which such subjects take on the nerves is extraordinary. Bulwer is in the hands of a set of mediums, and passes his time in conversation with his dead daughter. Mrs. Browning believes every word, and referred me to that very book for facts and proofs. The madhouses in America are overflowing with the victims of the delusion. "	http://www.browningscorrespondence.com/supporting-documents/2616/?rsId=16387	5/19/2014	GHB
